ORIGINAL REFERENCES: 
Huybers, P. (in press 2006).
Glacial variability over the last 2Ma: an extended depth-derived agemodel, 
continuous obliquity pacing, and the Pleistocene progression.  
Quaternary Science Reviews, in press.

Huybers, P. and Wunsch, C. 2004. 
A depth-derived Pleistocene age-model: 
uncertainty estimates, sedimentation variability, and nonlinear climate change.
Paleoceanography, v19, 10.1029/2002PA000857.  

Huybers, P. 2006. 
Early Pleistocene Glacial Cycles and the Integrated Summer Insolation Forcing. 
Science, v313, p508-511, 10.1126/science.1125249.


ABSTRACT (Huybers 2006 QSR):
An agemodel not relying upon orbital assumptions is estimated over the last 2Ma 
using depth in marine sediment cores as a proxy for time. Agemodel uncertainty 
averages ±10Ky in the early Pleistocene (~2 to 1Ma) and ±7Ky in the late Pleistocene 
(~1Ma to the present). Twelve benthic and five planktic delta-18O records are pinned 
to the agemodel and averaged together to provide a record of glacial variability. 
Major deglaciation features are identified over the last 2Ma and a remarkable 33 
out of 36 occur when Earth’s obliquity is anomalously large. During the early 
Pleistocene deglaciations occur nearly every obliquity cycle giving a 40Ky timescale, 
while late Pleistocene deglaciations more often skip one or two obliquity beats, 
corresponding to 80 or 120Ky glacial cycles which, on average, give the  100Ky 
variability. This continuous obliquity pacing indicates that the glacial theory 
can be simplified. An explanation for the  100Ky glacial cycles only requires a 
change in the likelihood of skipping an obliquity cycle, rather than a new sources 
of long-period variability. Furthermore, changes in glacial variability are not 
marked by any single transition so much as they exhibit a steady progression over 
the entire Pleistocene. The mean, variance, skewness, and time-scale associated 
with the glacial cycles all exhibit an approximately linear trend over the last 2Ma. 
A simple model having an obliquity modulated threshold and only three adjustable 
parameters is shown to reproduce the trends, timing, and spectral evolution 
associated with the Pleistocene glacial variability.

GEOGRAPHIC REGION: Global
PERIOD OF RECORD: 2.58 million years BP - present
FUNDING SOURCE: 
National Science Foundation (USA) Paleoclimate Program grant no. ATM-0455470.



DESCRIPTION:  
This collection of files provides the data used in constructing the extended 
depth-derived age-model, the resulting age-model and composite delta-18O records, 
the uncertainty estimates associated with the age-model, and files for comparing 
the age-model against other well-known estimates.  Age is estimated as a piece-wise 
linear function of depth, where the depth-scale is first corrected for down-core 
sediment compaction.  The most important feature of this age-model is that it does 
not rely upon orbital assumptions and thus should permit a more objective analysis 
of the relationship between climate variability and Earth's orbital variations 
than would an orbitally-tuned chronology. The extended depth-derived age-model 
spans the last 2.58 million years, and it is an extension of the depth-derived 
age-model of Huybers and Wunsch (2004) which spans only the last 0.78 million years.  
During the period of overlap, there are minor differences between the two age-models 
consistent with the use of more age-control points and a slightly different set of 
records in the extended depth-derived age-model (see the paper for details).  
The extended depth-derived age-model was also used in the study of Huybers (2006).

DATA:
1. Averaged delta18O records on the depth-derived age-model.  
Columns are depth-derived age, estimated one-standard-deviation agemodel
uncertainty, average of all delta18O records, average of the benthic
records, and average of the planktic records.  Averages are weighted
according to the sampling resolution of each record.

3. Event designations and age estimates.  The age estimate provided by
each sediment core is listed.  Ages are estimated by linearly
interpolating age with depth between geomagnetic events.  Note that
down-core depth is first corrected for sediment compaction.  "NaN"s
indicate that an event was not identifiable in the delta18O
stratigraphy.  In the case where more than one delta 18O record is
availalbe from a core, a "b" after the name indicates ages obtained
from benthic species and "p" is for planktic species.  At bottom the
standard deviation between ages is listed, the average age, and the
expected uncertainty of the average.  This expected uncertainty is
slightly larger than the standard deviation divided by the square-root
of the number of records owing to the presence of systematic
uncertainties in the age-model.

4. Marine sediment core delta-18O records.  Records are provided in the order
listed in table 1 of the paper, "Glacial variability over the last 2Ma: an
extended depth-derived agemodel, continuous obliquity pacing, and the
Pleistocene progression" (Huybers, 2006).  Columns are sediment core depth in
meters below sea-floor, uncompacted sediment core depth, age estimated by
linearly interpolating age with uncompacted depth in each core, age estimated
using the mean event ages across all cores (called depth-derived age), and the
delta-18O values.  See table~1 for references.  Ages are only provided up to
the oldest geomagnetic event present in each record.


The delta18O records listed below were made available by a large number of
investigators, and references for each record are provided below.  Future
studies using the below delta18O records should cite the original authors.


DSDP607: Ruddiman, W. F., Raymo, M., Martinson, D., Clement, B., Backman, J., 1989. 
Pleistocene evolution: Northern Hemisphere ice sheets and the North Atlantic Ocean. 
Paleoceanography 4, 353 412.

Quidelleur, X., Lancelot, Y., 1994.

A 1500-kyr record of carbonate dissolution. Paleoceanography 9, 579 599.

5.
Conversions from the published agemodels of Lisiecke and Raymo [2005],
Huybers and Wunsch [2004], and the Shackleton composite sequence to
the extended depth-derived age-model.  Columns are ordered as the
original agemodel, the agemodel adjusted to the extended depth-derived
agemodel, and the delta-18O record.  Ages are in kila-years before
present.

Age conversions are estimated using a simulated annealing approach.
Specifically, the agemodel of each record is adjusted so as to
maximize the cross-correlation between its delta-18O record and the
extended depth-derived delta18O stack.  Adjustments are made to the
ages associated with the events indicated in Figures~1 and 2 of the
manuscript, and in-between ages are linearly stretched or squeezed.



References:

1. Lisiecki and Raymo results (LR05):
(obtained from http://www.lorraine-lisiecki.com/stack.html)

Lisiecki, L. E., and M. E. Raymo, 2005 A Plio-Pleistocene stack of 57
globally distributed benthic d18O records, Paleoceanography,
doi:10.1029/2004PA001071.


2.  Late Pleistocene depth-derived results (HW04):

P. Huybers and C. Wunsch, 2004, A depth-derived Pleistocene age-model:
uncertainty estimates, sedimentation variability, and nonlinear
climate change, Paleoceanography.


3. Shackleton compostion sequence (Shack.):
(obtained from http://131.111.44.196/coredata/v677846.html)

the reference for the ODP 677 part of the timescale is:
Shackleton, N.J., Berger, A. and Peltier, W.R., 1990 An alternative
astronomical calibration of the lower Pleistocene timescale based on
ODP Site 677.  Trans. R. Soc. Edinburgh, Earth Sci.,81: 251-261

for the 846 isotope stratigraphy:
Shackleton, N.J., Hall,  M. A. and Pate,  D. 1995
Pliocene stable isotope stratigraphy of Site 846
Pisias, N.G., Janacek, L. A., Palmer-Julson, A., and Van Andel, T.H. (Eds.)
Proceedings of the Ocean Drilling Program, Scientific Results, vol. 138: 337-355

for the 846 agemodel: 
Shackleton, N.J., Crowhurst, S., Hagelberg, T., Pisias, N.G., and
Schneider, D.A., 1995 A new Late Neogene time scale: application to
Leg 138 Sites Pisias, N.G., Janacek, L. A., Palmer-Julson, A., and Van
Andel, T.H. (Eds.)  Proceedings of the Ocean Drilling Program,
Scientific Results, vol. 138: 73-101

for V1930: 
Shackleton, N.J. and Pisias, N.G., 1985 Atmospheric carbon dioxide,
orbital forcing, and climate in: E.T Sundquist and W.S. Broeker (Eds.)
The Carbon cycle and atmospheric CO2: natural variations Archean to
present Geophysical Monograph 32, 412-417
